ChatGPT

🔥 Phase 1: Getting OrcaSlicer Running in the Cloud

First, we need to containerize OrcaSlicer and get it running on Google Cloud. Since OrcaSlicer is a fork of PrusaSlicer and based on C++, we’ll need to build it in a cloud-friendly way while ensuring it can handle slicing requests via API.

Step 1: Containerizing OrcaSlicer

We’ll wrap OrcaSlicer’s CLI into a Docker container, enabling us to deploy it to Google Cloud Run or Kubernetes (GKE).



💾 Dockerfile for OrcaSlicer Cloud Build

dockerfile

CopyEdit

FROM ubuntu:22.04



# Install dependencies

RUN apt-get update && apt-get install -y \

git cmake g++ python3 python3-pip \

libboost-all-dev libgtk-3-dev libglu1-mesa-dev \

libglew-dev libxmu-dev libxi-dev \

libcurl4-openssl-dev libssl-dev \

&& rm -rf /var/lib/apt/lists/*



# Clone OrcaSlicer source

WORKDIR /app

RUN git clone --recursive https://github.com/SoftFever/OrcaSlicer.git && \

cd OrcaSlicer && mkdir build && cd build && \

cmake .. -DCMAKE_BUILD_TYPE=Release && make -j$(nproc)



# Set up a lightweight Flask API

RUN pip3 install flask

COPY server.py /app/server.py



# Expose API port

EXPOSE 5000

CMD ["python3", "/app/server.py"]

Step 2: Adding API for Remote Slicing

Since we’re running this in the cloud, we need an API to receive STL files, process them, and return G-code.



server.py – API Wrapper for OrcaSlicer

python

CopyEdit

from flask import Flask, request, jsonifyimport subprocessimport os



app = Flask(__name__)



UPLOAD_FOLDER = "/app/uploads"

PROCESSED_FOLDER = "/app/processed"

os.makedirs(UPLOAD_FOLDER, exist_ok=True)

os.makedirs(PROCESSED_FOLDER, exist_ok=True)@app.route('/slice', methods=['POST'])def slice_model():

file = request.files['stl']

if file:

filename = os.path.join(UPLOAD_FOLDER, file.filename)

file.save(filename)



# Run OrcaSlicer CLI to slice the file

gcode_filename = os.path.join(PROCESSED_FOLDER, file.filename.replace(".stl", ".gcode"))

cmd = f"/app/OrcaSlicer/build/bin/orcaslicer-cli {filename} -o {gcode_filename}"

subprocess.run(cmd, shell=True, check=True)



return jsonify({"gcode_url": f"/download/{gcode_filename}"}), 200@app.route('/download/<path:filename>', methods=['GET'])def download_file(filename):

return send_from_directory(PROCESSED_FOLDER, filename, as_attachment=True)if __name__ == '__main__':

app.run(host='0.0.0.0', port=5000)

Step 3: Deploy to Google Cloud

Now that we have the containerized version, we deploy it.



A. Build and Push to Google Container Registry

bash

CopyEdit

# Authenticate with Google Cloud

gcloud auth configure-docker# Build the Docker image

docker build -t gcr.io/YOUR_PROJECT_ID/orcaslicer-cloud .# Push the image to Google Container Registry

docker push gcr.io/YOUR_PROJECT_ID/orcaslicer-cloud

B. Deploy to Cloud Run

bash

CopyEdit

gcloud run deploy orcaslicer-cloud \

--image gcr.io/YOUR_PROJECT_ID/orcaslicer-cloud \

--platform managed \

--allow-unauthenticated \

--region us-west1 \

--memory 4Gi \

--cpu 2

🚀 Now you have a cloud-based OrcaSlicer running!

🔄 Step 4: Optimizing for Conical Slicing

Now that OrcaSlicer is cloud-enabled, we optimize it for conical slicing.



A. Understanding Conical Slicing

Problem: Traditional slicers use planar slicing, causing inefficiencies for rotationally symmetric models.

Solution: Conical slicing follows the nozzle’s geometry, improving overhang performance.

B. Modifying OrcaSlicer’s Geometry Calculations

Add a custom nozzle collision check.

Implement variable Z-layers following a conical path.

📐 Modification: Add Nozzle Geometry to Slicing Engine

Modify OrcaSlicer’s G-code generator to include nozzle clearance checks.

Modify GCodeProcessor.cpp:



cpp

CopyEdit

// Define nozzle geometrydouble nozzle_radius = 0.4; // Example: 0.4mm nozzledouble clearance_angle = 45.0; // Define conical angle// Modify layer height dynamically based on conical slicingdouble compute_layer_height(double radius) {

return radius * tan(clearance_angle * M_PI / 180.0);

}

github.com

Steps:

2. Adding Arc Overhang Capabilities

Arc overhangs enable printing 90° overhangs without support by generating concentric arc toolpaths. The Arc Overhang project provides a slicing algorithm for this purpose.

github.com

Steps:



Explore Arc Overhang Algorithm:

Study the Arc Overhang repository to understand its implementation.

Integrate into OrcaSlicer:

Adapt the arc overhang algorithm into your OrcaSlicer fork, ensuring it complements existing slicing strategies.

Provide User Options:

Allow users to enable or disable arc overhang slicing and adjust parameters as needed.

3. Implementing Brick Layer Patterns

Brick layer patterns enhance print strength by offsetting layers, similar to bricklaying. A recent open-source post-processor script introduces this technique.

hackaday.com

Steps:



Review Brick Layer Script:

Examine the Brick Layer Post-Processor to understand its functionality.

Integrate into OrcaSlicer:

Incorporate the brick layer pattern logic into your slicer's post-processing phase.

User Configuration:

Actions:

CopyEdit

Review the main script (slicer4rtn) to understand how it processes STL files and generates conical slices.

Understand the Workflow:

Slicer4RTN's workflow involves:Subdividing the Model: Increasing mesh resolution for better slicing accuracy.

Inverse Conic Mapping: Transforming the model into an inverse conic space.

Slicing with a Planar Slicer: Using tools like Slic3r to generate G-code.

Direct Conic Mapping: Transforming the G-code back to the conic space.

Adding Rotational Commands: Incorporating rotation commands for the tilted nozzle.

Identify Key Functions:

Focus on functions responsible for:Conic space mapping.

G-code transformation.

Rotation command insertion.

Phase 2: Implementing Conical Slicing - Core Logic

Now comes the crucial part – modifying the code. We'll focus on integrating the core concepts of conical slicing, drawing inspiration from Slicer4RTN but adapting it to OrcaSlicer's architecture.

Understanding Slicer4RTN's Approach (Quick Recap):

Inverse Conic Mapping: Transforms the 3D model into a space where slicing can be done in a conical manner.
Planar Slicing: Uses a standard planar slicer (like Slic3r, which OrcaSlicer is based on) on the transformed model.
Direct Conic Mapping: Transforms the resulting G-code back to the original coordinate space.
Rotation Commands: Adds G-code commands to rotate the print bed (or nozzle, in a 4-axis setup) to achieve the conical layers.
Locating the Relevant Code in OrcaSlicer:

OrcaSlicer's slicing engine is heavily based on Slic3r. We need to find where the layer generation and toolpath planning happen.
Key files to look at (this might require some exploration):
src/libslic3r/Layer.cpp
src/libslic3r/Layer.hpp
src/libslic3r/GCode.cpp
src/libslic3r/GCode.hpp
src/libslic3r/Print.cpp
src/libslic3r/Print.hpp
src/libslic3r/PrintConfig.hpp (and related configuration files)
Adding a Conical Slicing Toggle:

First, let's add a simple setting to enable/disable conical slicing. This will help us isolate our modifications.

C++

Rebuild orcaslicer and you should see the new conical slicing option in the "advanced" tab under print settings.
Implementing Conical Layer Heights:

In src/libslic3r/Layer.cpp (or similar):
We need to modify how layer heights are calculated. Instead of uniform Z steps, we'll base the height on a conical angle.
Find the part of the code where layer_height or a similar variable is determined.
Add a conditional check for our new setting:
C++

// ... existing layer height calculation ...

if (print_config.enable_conical_slicing) {
    // Calculate layer height based on a cone angle
    // (This is a simplified example - you'll need to refine the math)
    double cone_angle = 45.0; // Degrees (make this a configurable option later)
    double radius = current_x_position; // Example - get the radius from the model 
    layer_height = radius * tan(cone_angle * M_PI / 180.0);
} 

// ... rest of the layer height logic ...
Important: The above is a very basic illustration. You'll need to:
Determine the correct radius (distance from the center axis). This will likely involve analyzing the model's geometry at each layer.
Refine the cone_angle calculation or make it a user-configurable parameter.
Handle edge cases (e.g., what happens when the radius is very small or zero?).
Adjusting Toolpaths for Conical Layers:

In src/libslic3r/GCode.cpp (or where toolpaths are generated):
We need to modify the X, Y, and Z coordinates in the G-code to follow the conical path.
This will likely involve adding transformations (similar to Slicer4RTN's conic mappings) to the coordinates before they are written to the G-code.
Example (Conceptual):
C++

// ... inside the G-code generation loop ...

if (print_config.enable_conical_slicing) {
    // Apply a transformation to x, y based on current layer height and cone angle
    double transformed_x = x * cos(current_layer_height / cone_radius); 
    double transformed_y = y * sin(current_layer_height / cone_radius);

    // Use transformed_x and transformed_y in the G-code output
    gcode += "G1 X" + to_string(transformed_x) + " Y" + to_string(transformed_y) + " Z" + to_string(current_layer_height) + "\n";
} else {
    // ... regular G-code generation ...
    gcode += "G1 X" + to_string(x) + " Y" + to_string(y) + " Z" + to_string(current_layer_height) + "\n";
}

// ...
Note:
cone_radius is a placeholder. You'll need to determine this based on your model and desired cone shape.
The transformation math will need careful consideration and likely involve more complex calculations than the simple example above.
Adding Rotation Commands (Optional):

If you're targeting a 4-axis printer or want to simulate conical slicing on a 3-axis printer by rotating the print bed, you'll need to insert appropriate rotation commands (e.g., G68 or custom G-codes) in the G-code output.
This would be done in the G-code generation section, similar to the toolpath adjustments.

Phase 4: Completing Conical Slicing Integration

Refine the Conical Transformation Math:

The examples I provided earlier were simplified. You'll need to implement more robust mathematical transformations to accurately map between planar and conical coordinates.
Key Considerations:
Center of the Cone: Determine the central axis of the conical layers. This might be the center of the object or a user-defined point.
Cone Angle: Calculate the angle of the cone dynamically based on the model's geometry or allow user input.
Radius: Accurately calculate the radius at each layer height.
Resources:
Research papers on conical slicing and non-planar 3D printing.
Geometric transformations in 3D graphics.
Implement Nozzle Collision Detection:

This is crucial to prevent the nozzle from crashing into previously printed parts.
Approach:
Define Nozzle Geometry: Create a representation of your nozzle's shape (e.g., a cylinder or cone).
Collision Checks: During toolpath generation, check if the nozzle's geometry would intersect with any already-printed parts at the current layer and previous layers.
Adjust Toolpaths: If a collision is detected, modify the toolpath (e.g., retract, move around the obstacle, or adjust the layer height).
Libraries: You might find libraries like libigl or CGAL helpful for collision detection, but integrating them into OrcaSlicer could be a significant effort. Consider starting with a simpler, custom implementation.
Handle Edge Cases:

Very Small Radii: Determine how to handle layers where the radius is smaller than the nozzle's radius or close to zero. You might need to switch to planar slicing or use a special strategy for these areas.
Sharp Corners: Conical slicing might introduce artifacts at sharp corners. Consider smoothing or rounding techniques.
Model Orientation: How should the orientation of the model affect the conical slicing? Should the user be able to choose the axis of the cone?
Thorough Testing:

Variety of Models: Test with a wide range of models, including those with:
Steep overhangs
Hollow sections
Fine details
Combinations of curved and flat surfaces

Test Prints: Conduct numerous test prints to validate the real-world results.

Create user-friendly controls in the OrcaSlicer interface to:
Enable/disable conical slicing.
Set the cone angle (or have it automatically calculated).
Define the center of the cone.
Adjust nozzle geometry parameters.
Potentially visualize the nozzle's path during slicing.

API Endpoint (server.py):

Python

from flask import Flask, request, jsonify
from google.cloud import aiplatform

# ... other imports ...

app = Flask(__name__)

# Initialize Vertex AI client (do this outside the request handler for better performance)
aiplatform.init(project=YOUR_PROJECT_ID, location=YOUR_REGION)
endpoint = aiplatform.Endpoint(ENDPOINT_ID)

@app.route('/slice', methods=['POST'])
def slice_model():
    # ... handle file upload and basic slicing ...

    if request.args.get('use_gemini'):
        try:
            # Prepare input for Gemini (e.g., extract features from the STL)
            features = extract_features(stl_file_path) 

            # Call Vertex AI Endpoint for prediction
            response = endpoint.predict(instances=[features])
            predictions = response.predictions[0]

            # Use predictions to adjust slicing parameters
            layer_height = predictions['layer_height']
            infill_density = predictions['infill_density']
            # ...

        except Exception as e:
            return jsonify({'error': f'Error calling Gemini: {e}'}), 500

    # ... continue with slicing using the adjusted parameters ...
Feature Extraction (extract_features function):

This function would analyze the STL file and extract relevant features that your Gemini model expects (e.g., volume, surface area, bounding box dimensions, number of vertices, etc.). You might use libraries like trimesh or numpy-stl for this.

// In ConicalSlicer.cpp
#include "ConicalSlicer.hpp"
#include "Layer.hpp"
#include "GCode.hpp"
#include <cmath>

namespace Slic3r {

Point3 ConicalSlicer::transformToConical(const Point3& point, double cone_angle, double center_x, double center_y) {
    double radius = std::sqrt(std::pow(point.x - center_x, 2) + std::pow(point.y - center_y, 2));
    double layer_height = radius * std::tan(cone_angle * M_PI / 180.0);

    // ... more sophisticated transformation logic here ...

    return Point3(transformed_x, transformed_y, layer_height);
}

// ... other methods for applying the transformation during slicing ...

}

from flask import Flask, request, jsonify
from google.cloud import tasks_v2
import json

app = Flask(__name__)
tasks_client = tasks_v2.CloudTasksClient()

# ...

@app.route('/slice', methods=['POST'])
def slice_model():
    # ... input validation ...

    task = {
        'app_engine_http_request': {
            'http_method': 'POST',
            'relative_uri': '/process_slice_job',  # Internal endpoint for processing
            'body': json.dumps({'stl_path': stl_path, 'params': slicing_params}).encode(), # Payload
            'headers': {'Content-type': 'application/json'}
        }
    }

    # set the project, queue, and location variables to your own
    parent = tasks_client.queue_path(project=YOUR_PROJECT, location=YOUR_REGION, queue=YOUR_QUEUE)
    response = tasks_client.create_task(parent=parent, task=task)

    return jsonify({'task_id': response.name}), 202

# ... (Add a new endpoint `/process_slice_job` to handle the actual slicing) ...

from flask import Flask, request, jsonify
from google.cloud import aiplatform
from google.cloud import storage
import json
import os
import subprocess

# ... other imports ...

app = Flask(__name__)

# Initialize Vertex AI and Storage Clients outside of the request handler for efficiency
aiplatform.init(project=YOUR_PROJECT_ID, location=YOUR_REGION)
endpoint = aiplatform.Endpoint(endpoint_id=ENDPOINT_ID)
storage_client = storage.Client()

# ... (Other parts of your server.py, including the Cloud Tasks setup) ...

@app.route('/slice', methods=['POST'])
def slice_model():
    """Handles file upload, optional Gemini integration, and triggers slicing."""
    try:
        file = request.files['stl']
        if not file:
            return jsonify({'error': 'No STL file provided'}), 400

        # 1. Upload STL to Google Cloud Storage
        bucket = storage_client.bucket(YOUR_BUCKET_NAME)
        blob = bucket.blob(file.filename)
        blob.upload_from_file(file)
        stl_path = f"gs://{YOUR_BUCKET_NAME}/{file.filename}"

        # 2. Prepare Slicing Parameters (with optional Gemini suggestions)
        slicing_params = {
            'layer_height': request.form.get('layer_height', 0.2),  # Default values
            'infill_density': request.form.get('infill_density', 20),
            # ... other parameters ...
            'enable_conical_slicing': request.form.get('enable_conical_slicing', False),
            # ...
        }

        if request.form.get('use_gemini'):
            try:
                # Extract features for Gemini
                features = extract_features(stl_path)

                # Call Vertex AI Endpoint for prediction
                response = endpoint.predict(instances=[features])
                predictions = response.predictions[0]

                # Update slicing parameters based on predictions
                slicing_params['layer_height'] = predictions.get('layer_height', slicing_params['layer_height'])
                slicing_params['infill_density'] = predictions.get('infill_density', slicing_params['infill_density'])
                # ... update other parameters ...

            except Exception as e:
                print(f"Error calling Gemini: {e}")  # Log the error
                # Optionally, handle the error gracefully (e.g., use default parameters)

        # 3. Enqueue Slicing Task to Cloud Tasks
        task = {
            'app_engine_http_request': {
                'http_method': 'POST',
                'relative_uri': '/process_slice_job',  # Internal endpoint for processing
                'body': json.dumps({'stl_path': stl_path, 'params': slicing_params}).encode(),
                'headers': {'Content-type': 'application/json'}
            }
        }

        parent = tasks_client.queue_path(project=YOUR_PROJECT_ID, location=YOUR_REGION, queue=YOUR_QUEUE)
        response = tasks_client.create_task(parent=parent, task=task)

        return jsonify({'task_id': response.name}), 202

    except Exception as e:
        return jsonify({'error': f'An unexpected error occurred: {e}'}), 500

@app.route('/process_slice_job', methods=['POST'])
def process_slice_job():
    """Internal endpoint to perform the actual slicing (triggered by Cloud Tasks)."""
    try:
        payload = json.loads(request.data.decode())
        stl_path = payload['stl_path']
        slicing_params = payload['params']

        # 1. Download STL from Cloud Storage
        bucket_name = stl_path.split('/')[2]
        blob_name = stl_path.split('/', 3)[3]
        
        bucket = storage_client.get_bucket(bucket_name)
        blob = bucket.blob(blob_name)
        
        local_stl_path = f"/tmp/{os.path.basename(stl_path)}"  # Use /tmp in Cloud Run
        blob.download_to_filename(local_stl_path)

        # 2. Perform Slicing with OrcaSlicer CLI (and your modifications)
        gcode_path = local_stl_path.replace(".stl", ".gcode")
        
        # Construct the OrcaSlicer CLI command
        command = [
            "./orcaslicer",  # Assuming OrcaSlicer binary is in the root of the app
            local_stl_path,
            "-o", gcode_path,
            "--layer-height", str(slicing_params['layer_height']),
            "--infill-density", str(slicing_params['infill_density']),
            # ... add other parameters, including conical slicing, arc overhangs, etc. ...
        ]
        
        if slicing_params['enable_conical_slicing']:
            command.append("--enable-conical-slicing") #add additional options as needed, ensure the names match what you have in your source code
            # ... add other conical slicing parameters ...
            
        # Execute the command
        subprocess.run(command, check=True)

        # 3. Upload G-code to Cloud Storage
        gcode_blob = bucket.blob(os.path.basename(gcode_path))
        gcode_blob.upload_from_filename(gcode_path)

        # 4. Clean up local files (Cloud Run is ephemeral)
        os.remove(local_stl_path)
        os.remove(gcode_path)

        return jsonify({'gcode_url': f"gs://{YOUR_BUCKET_NAME}/{os.path.basename(gcode_path)}"}), 200

    except Exception as e:
        print(f"Error during slicing: {e}")  # Log the error
        return jsonify({'error': f'Slicing failed: {e}'}), 500

# ... (Rest of your server.py) ...

import os
import subprocess
import sys
import json
from setuptools import setup, Extension
from setuptools.command.build_ext import build_ext

# --- Configuration (Adjust these to match your project) ---
PROJECT_ID = "YOUR_GOOGLE_CLOUD_PROJECT_ID"  # Replace with your GCP project ID
BUCKET_NAME = "YOUR_CLOUD_STORAGE_BUCKET_NAME"  # Replace with your bucket name
REGION = "us-central1"  # Replace with your preferred region
ENDPOINT_ID = "YOUR_VERTEX_AI_ENDPOINT_ID" # Replace with your Vertex AI Endpoint ID (if using Gemini)
ORCASLICER_REPO = "https://github.com/YOUR_USERNAME/OrcaSlicer.git"  # Your forked OrcaSlicer repo
DOCKER_IMAGE_NAME = "gcr.io/" + PROJECT_ID + "/orcaslicer-cloud"
CLOUD_RUN_SERVICE_NAME = "orcaslicer-cloud"
TASK_QUEUE_NAME = "orcaslicer-queue"

# --- Conical Slicing Calculations (Example - Adapt to your implementation) ---
CONE_ANGLE = 45.0  # Default cone angle (in degrees)
NOZZLE_DIAMETER = 0.4  # Example nozzle diameter

def conical_transform(x, y, z, cone_angle=CONE_ANGLE, center_x=0.0, center_y=0.0):
    """
    Transforms Cartesian coordinates to conical coordinates.

    Args:
        x: X-coordinate.
        y: Y-coordinate.
        z: Z-coordinate (original layer height).
        cone_angle: Angle of the cone (in degrees).
        center_x: X-coordinate of the cone's center.
        center_y: Y-coordinate of the cone's center.

    Returns:
        A tuple containing the transformed (x, y, z) coordinates.
    """
    radius = (x**2 + y**2)**0.5
    # Calculate the new layer height based on the cone angle
    conical_z = radius * math.tan(math.radians(cone_angle))

    # Simple transformation (adjust this based on your specific algorithm)
    # This example keeps x and y unchanged, but you'll likely modify them
    # based on the cone's geometry and the desired toolpath.
    transformed_x = x
    transformed_y = y

    return transformed_x, transformed_y, conical_z


def conical_layer_height(radius, cone_angle=CONE_ANGLE):
    """
    Calculates the layer height for conical slicing based on the radius.

    Args:
        radius: Distance from the center axis.
        cone_angle: Angle of the cone (in degrees).

    Returns:
        The calculated layer height.
    """
    return radius * math.tan(math.radians(cone_angle))

# --- Helper Functions ---

def run_command(command, capture_output=False):
    """Runs a shell command."""
    print(f"Running command: {' '.join(command)}")
    try:
        if capture_output:
            result = subprocess.run(command, check=True, capture_output=True, text=True)
            return result.stdout.strip()
        else:
            subprocess.run(command, check=True)
    except subprocess.CalledProcessError as e:
        print(f"Error running command: {e}")
        sys.exit(1)

def gcloud(*args):
    """Runs a gcloud command."""
    return run_command(["gcloud"] + list(args))

def gsutil(*args):
    """Runs a gsutil command."""
    return run_command(["gsutil"] + list(args))

def docker(*args):
    """Runs a docker command."""
    return run_command(["docker"] + list(args))

# --- Setup Steps ---

def setup_google_cloud():
    """Sets up Google Cloud resources."""
    print("Setting up Google Cloud...")

    # 1. Create Project (if it doesn't exist)
    # (You might need to handle this manually if you don't have project creation permissions)
    try:
        gcloud("projects", "describe", PROJECT_ID)
        print(f"Project {PROJECT_ID} already exists.")
    except subprocess.CalledProcessError:
        print(f"Creating project {PROJECT_ID}...")
        gcloud("projects", "create", PROJECT_ID)

    # 2. Enable APIs
    gcloud("services", "enable",
           "compute.googleapis.com",
           "containerregistry.googleapis.com",
           "run.googleapis.com",
           "storage.googleapis.com",
           "cloudbuild.googleapis.com",
           "aiplatform.googleapis.com",
           "sqladmin.googleapis.com", # if using CloudSQL
           "tasks.googleapis.com"
          )

    # 3. Create Storage Bucket
    try:
        gsutil("mb", "-l", REGION, f"gs://{BUCKET_NAME}")
    except subprocess.CalledProcessError as e:
        if "409" in str(e):
            print(f"Bucket {BUCKET_NAME} already exists.")
        else:
            raise

    # 4. Create Cloud Tasks Queue
    try:
        gcloud("tasks", "queues", "describe", TASK_QUEUE_NAME, "--location", REGION)
        print(f"Cloud Tasks queue {TASK_QUEUE_NAME} already exists.")
    except subprocess.CalledProcessError:
        print(f"Creating Cloud Tasks queue {TASK_QUEUE_NAME}...")
        gcloud("tasks", "queues", "create", TASK_QUEUE_NAME, "--location", REGION)
    
    # 5. Create Vertex AI Endpoint (if using Gemini and you have already trained your model)
    # If not using Gemini you can remove this
    try:
        gcloud("ai", "endpoints", "describe", ENDPOINT_ID, "--region", REGION)
    except subprocess.CalledProcessError:
        print(f"Creating Vertex AI Endpoint: {ENDPOINT_ID} has not been created by your account")

def build_orcaslicer():
    """Clones, modifies, and builds OrcaSlicer."""
    print("Building OrcaSlicer...")

    # 1. Clone OrcaSlicer
    if not os.path.exists("OrcaSlicer"):
        run_command(["git", "clone", "--recursive", ORCASLICER_REPO])

    # 2. Modify OrcaSlicer (Apply Patches or Manual Edits)
    # (This is where you would integrate your conical slicing, arc overhangs, etc.)
    # Example: Applying a patch file
    # run_command(["git", "apply", "path/to/your/conical_slicing.patch"])

    # Example: Manual edits (Illustrative)
    #   - Modify src/libslic3r/Layer.cpp to incorporate conical layer height calculations
    #   - Modify src/libslic3r/GCode.cpp to apply conical transformations
    #   - ... other modifications ...

    # 3. Build OrcaSlicer
    os.chdir("OrcaSlicer")
    if sys.platform == "win32":
        # Windows build (using build.bat)
        # Ensure you have Visual Studio, CMake, and other dependencies installed
        run_command(["./build.bat"])  # Or the appropriate build command for your setup
    else:
        # Linux/macOS build (using build.sh)
        run_command(["./build.sh"])
    os.chdir("..")

def create_dockerfile():
    """Creates the Dockerfile for the application."""
    print("Creating Dockerfile...")
    dockerfile_content = f"""
# Use a multi-stage build to reduce image size

# Stage 1: Build OrcaSlicer
FROM ubuntu:22.04 AS build-env

# Install dependencies
RUN apt-get update && apt-get install -y \\
    git cmake g++ python3 python3-pip \\
    libboost-all-dev libgtk-3-dev libglu1-mesa-dev \\
    libglew-dev libxmu-dev libxi-dev \\
    libcurl4-openssl-dev libssl-dev \\
    libtbb-dev \\
    libgdal-dev \\
    libglm-dev \\
    libeigen3-dev \\
    libdbus-1-dev \\
    libncurses-dev \\
    libjsoncpp-dev \\
    libcereal-dev \\
    libdate-dev \\
    libgeographic-dev \\
    libgeographic-doc \\
    libopenssl-dev \\
    libwxgtk3.0-gtk3-dev

# Clone OrcaSlicer source
WORKDIR /app/src
RUN git clone --recursive {ORCASLICER_REPO} 

# Set up the build environment and compile
WORKDIR /app/src/OrcaSlicer
RUN mkdir build && cd build && \\
    cmake .. -DCMAKE_BUILD_TYPE=Release && \\
    cmake --build . --config Release --target install -- -j$(nproc)

# Stage 2: Create the runtime image
FROM debian:buster-slim

# Set working directory
WORKDIR /app

# Copy only necessary files from the build stage
COPY --from=build-env /app/src/OrcaSlicer/build/bin /app/bin
COPY --from=build-env /app/src/OrcaSlicer/resources /app/resources
COPY server.py /app/
COPY requirements.txt /app/

# Install Python dependencies
RUN pip install --no-cache-dir -r requirements.txt

# Expose the port the app runs on
EXPOSE 5000

# Command to run the application
CMD ["python", "server.py"]
"""
    with open("Dockerfile", "w") as f:
        f.write(dockerfile_content)

def build_and_push_docker_image():
    """Builds and pushes the Docker image to Google Container Registry."""
    print("Building and pushing Docker image...")

    # 1. Build Docker Image
    docker("build", "-t", DOCKER_IMAGE_NAME, ".")

    # 2. Configure Docker for Google Cloud (if not already done)
    gcloud("auth", "configure-docker")

    # 3. Push Docker Image
    docker("push", DOCKER_IMAGE_NAME)

def deploy_to_cloud_run():
    """Deploys the application to Google Cloud Run."""
    print("Deploying to Cloud Run...")
    gcloud("run", "deploy", CLOUD_RUN_SERVICE_NAME,
           "--image", DOCKER_IMAGE_NAME,
           "--platform", "managed",
           "--allow-unauthenticated",
           "--region", REGION,
           "--memory", "4Gi",  # Adjust as needed
           "--cpu", "2",  # Adjust as needed
           "--update-env-vars", f"GS_BUCKET={BUCKET_NAME},ENDPOINT_ID={ENDPOINT_ID}"
          )

# --- Main Setup ---

class CustomBuildExtCommand(build_ext):
    """Custom build command to perform extra steps."""
    def run(self):
        # 1. Setup Google Cloud
        setup_google_cloud()

        # 2. Build OrcaSlicer (with modifications)
        build_orcaslicer()

        # 3. Create Dockerfile
        create_dockerfile()

        # 4. Build and Push Docker Image
        build_and_push_docker_image()

        # 5. Deploy to Cloud Run
        deploy_to_cloud_run()

        # (Optional) Call the original build_ext command if needed
        # build_ext.run(self)

setup(

    version="0.1",
    description="Cloud-based OrcaSlicer with AI enhancements",
    # ... other setup parameters ...
    cmdclass={
        'build_ext': CustomBuildExtCommand,
    },
)

2.0 Experimental Advanced. Lacks access to real-time info and some Gemini features.
Okay, I understand. We're going to focus on modifying OrcaSlicer to integrate conical slicing, while leveraging the existing Bambu Lab printer profiles within OrcaSlicer. We'll prioritize getting the conical slicing logic working for these profiles first.

Here's a breakdown of the steps, combining code examples with explanations:

Phase 1: Setting Up and Understanding the Bambu Lab Profiles

Fork and Clone OrcaSlicer:

Fork the OrcaSlicer repository on GitHub: https://github.com/SoftFever/OrcaSlicer
Clone your fork locally:
Bash

git clone https://github.com/YOUR_USERNAME/OrcaSlicer.git
cd OrcaSlicer
Locate Bambu Lab Profiles:

The Bambu Lab printer profiles are located in the resources/profiles/BBL directory within the OrcaSlicer repository.
These profiles are defined in JSON files (e.g., Bambu Lab X1 Carbon 0.4 nozzle.json).
Understand Profile Structure:

Examine the JSON files to understand the structure of the Bambu Lab profiles.
Key parameters to look for:
bed_model: Path to the 3D model of the print bed.
bed_texture: Path to the texture for the print bed.
default_print_profile: Default print profile to use.
nozzle_diameter: Nozzle diameter.
printable_height: Maximum printable height.
printer_model: Printer model (e.g., "X1 Carbon").
printer_settings_id: Unique ID for the printer settings.
printer_technology: "FFF" (Fused Filament Fabrication).
thumbnails: Paths to thumbnail images.
vendor: "Bambu Lab".
version: Profile version.
Phase 2: Implementing Conical Slicing Logic

Create ConicalSlicer Class (C++):

In the src/libslic3r directory, create two new files: ConicalSlicer.hpp and ConicalSlicer.cpp.
ConicalSlicer.hpp:
C++

#ifndef CONICALSLICER_HPP
#define CONICALSLICER_HPP

#include "libslic3r.h"
#include "Point.hpp"

namespace Slic3r {

class ConicalSlicer {
public:
    ConicalSlicer();
    ~ConicalSlicer();

    // Main function to perform conical slicing on a layer
    void sliceLayerConically(Layer* layer, const PrintConfig& print_config);

private:
    // Helper functions for coordinate transformations
    Point3 transformToConical(const Point3& point, double cone_angle, double center_x, double center_y);
    double calculateConicalLayerHeight(double radius, double cone_angle);

    // ... other helper functions for nozzle collision detection, etc. ...
};

#endif // CONICALSLICER_HPP
ConicalSlicer.cpp:
C++

#include "ConicalSlicer.hpp"
#include "Layer.hpp"
#include "GCode.hpp"
#include "Print.hpp"
#include "Geometry.hpp"
#include <cmath>

namespace Slic3r {

ConicalSlicer::ConicalSlicer() {}
ConicalSlicer::~ConicalSlicer() {}

void ConicalSlicer::sliceLayerConically(Layer* layer, const PrintConfig& print_config) {
    // Get parameters from the PrintConfig (or elsewhere)
    double cone_angle = print_config.getParameter<double>("cone_angle"); // Example - get from config
    double center_x = print_config.getParameter<double>("cone_center_x");
    double center_y = print_config.getParameter<double>("cone_center_y");

    // Iterate through regions in the layer
    for (auto region : layer->regions()) {
        // Iterate through perimeters
        for (auto perimeter : region->perimeters()) {
          // Iterate through each point of the perimeter
          for (auto& point : perimeter->points) {
              //transform each point
              Point3 transformed_point = transformToConical(Point3(point.x, point.y, layer->height), cone_angle, center_x, center_y);

              // Update the point in the perimeter
              point.x = transformed_point.x();
              point.y = transformed_point.y();
          }
        }
     }

     //adjust layer height using helper function
     layer->height = calculateConicalLayerHeight(radius, cone_angle);

     // ... other operations on the layer (infill, supports, etc.) ...
     // ... nozzle collision detection and toolpath adjustments ...
}

Point3 ConicalSlicer::transformToConical(const Point3& point, double cone_angle, double center_x, double center_y) {
    double radius = std::sqrt(std::pow(point.x - center_x, 2) + std::pow(point.y - center_y, 2));
    double conical_z = point.z + calculateConicalLayerHeight(radius, cone_angle);

    // Example transformation (adjust this based on your algorithm)
    double transformed_x = point.x;
    double transformed_y = point.y;

    return Point3(transformed_x, transformed_y, conical_z);
}

double ConicalSlicer::calculateConicalLayerHeight(double radius, double cone_angle) {
    return radius * std::tan(Geometry::deg2rad(cone_angle));
}

} // namespace Slic3r

1. ConicalSlicer Class Enhancement

Header File (ConicalSlicer.hpp):
C++

#ifndef CONICALSLICER_HPP
#define CONICALSLICER_HPP

#include "libslic3r.h"
#include "Point.hpp"
#include "PrintConfig.hpp"

namespace Slic3r {

class Layer; // Forward declaration

class ConicalSlicer {
public:
    ConicalSlicer();
    ~ConicalSlicer();

    // Main function to perform conical slicing on a layer
    void sliceLayerConically(Layer* layer, const PrintConfig& print_config);

private:
    // Helper functions for coordinate transformations
    Point3 transformToConical(const Point3& point, double cone_angle, double center_x, double center_y, double layer_height_mm);
    double calculateConicalLayerHeight(double radius, double cone_angle);
    // Function to adjust extrusion for conical layers
    void adjustExtrusion(ExtrusionLoop* loop, double scale_factor);

    // Nozzle geometry for collision detection (example - might need a more complex representation)
    double nozzle_radius; 
};

} // namespace Slic3r

#endif // CONICALSLICER_HPP
Implementation File (ConicalSlicer.cpp):
C++

    #include "ConicalSlicer.hpp"
    #include "Layer.hpp"
    #include "GCode.hpp"
    #include "Print.hpp"
    #include "Geometry.hpp"
    #include <cmath>

    namespace Slic3r {

    ConicalSlicer::ConicalSlicer() : nozzle_radius(0.2) {} // Initialize nozzle radius
    ConicalSlicer::~ConicalSlicer() {}

    void ConicalSlicer::sliceLayerConically(Layer* layer, const PrintConfig& print_config) {
        // Retrieve conical slicing parameters
        double cone_angle = print_config.opt_float("cone_angle");
        double center_x = print_config.opt_float("cone_center_x");
        double center_y = print_config.opt_float("cone_center_y");
        
        // Adjust layer height based on the conical method
        // Iterate through regions in the layer
        for (auto region : layer->regions()) {
            // Iterate through perimeters
            for (auto perimeter : region->perimeters()) {
              // Iterate through each point of the perimeter
              for (size_t i = 0; i < perimeter->points.size(); ++i) {
                  auto& point = perimeter->points[i];
                  //transform each point
                  Point3 transformed_point = transformToConical(Point3(point.x, point.y, layer->height), cone_angle, center_x, center_y, layer->height);

                  // Update the point in the perimeter
                  point.x = transformed_point.x();
                  point.y = transformed_point.y();
                  
                  // Adjust the layer height based on the transformation
                  double radius = std::sqrt(std::pow(point.x - center_x, 2) + std::pow(point.y - center_y, 2));
                  layer->height = calculateConicalLayerHeight(radius, cone_angle);
              }
              
              // Adjust extrusion for this perimeter
              double scale_factor = std::cos(Geometry::deg2rad(cone_angle)); // Example scale factor
              for (auto& loop : perimeter->loops) {
                adjustExtrusion(&loop, scale_factor);
              }
            }
            
            // Similar adjustments can be made for infill and supports
            for (auto& infill : region->infill_paths()) {
                for (auto& point : infill.points) {
                    Point3 transformed_point = transformToConical(Point3(point.x, point.y, layer->height), cone_angle, center_x, center_y, layer->height);
                    point.x = transformed_point.x();
                    point.y = transformed_point.y();
                }
            }
        }
    }

    Point3 ConicalSlicer::transformToConical(const Point3& point, double cone_angle, double center_x, double center_y, double layer_height_mm) {
        // Calculate radius from the center
        double radius = std::sqrt(std::pow(point.x - center_x, 2) + std::pow(point.y - center_y, 2));

        // Calculate conical layer height
        double conical_z = layer_height_mm + calculateConicalLayerHeight(radius, cone_angle);

        // Keep x and y unchanged for this example, but you'll likely modify them
        // based on the cone's geometry and the desired toolpath.
        double transformed_x = point.x;
        double transformed_y = point.y;

        return Point3(transformed_x, transformed_y, conical_z);
    }

    double ConicalSlicer::calculateConicalLayerHeight(double radius, double cone_angle) {
        // Calculate the layer height based on the cone angle and radius
        return radius * std::tan(Geometry::deg2rad(cone_angle));
    }

    void ConicalSlicer::adjustExtrusion(ExtrusionLoop* loop, double scale_factor) {
        // Adjust the extrusion for each segment of the loop
        for (auto& segment : loop->segments) {
            segment.e *= scale_factor; // Adjust extrusion amount
        }
    }
    } // namespace Slic3r

namespace Slic3r {

// ... other methods ...
void Print::process_layers_conically(const PrintConfig& config) {
    if (config.opt_bool("enable_conical_slicing")) {
        // Create a ConicalSlicer instance
        ConicalSlicer conical_slicer;

        // Iterate over all layers and apply conical slicing
        for (Layer* layer : m_layers) {
            conical_slicer.sliceLayerConically(layer, config);
        }
    }
}

//modify the existing process_layer function
void Print::process_layers()
{
    // ... existing slicing logic ...

    // Perform conical slicing if enabled
    if (m_config.enable_conical_slicing.value)
    {
        //create and use the conical slicer instance
        ConicalSlicer conical_slicer;
        for (auto layer : m_layers)
        {
            conical_slicer.sliceLayerConically(layer, m_config);
        }
    }

    // ... rest of the slicing logic ...
}

} // namespace Slic3r

void PrintConfig::apply(const PrintConfig &other, bool ignore_nonexistent)

    this->option<ConfigOptionFloatOrBuild>(cone_angle)->value            = other.option<ConfigOptionFloatOrBuild>(cone_angle)->value;

    this->option<ConfigOptionFloatOrBuild>(cone_center_y)->value         = other.option<ConfigOptionFloatOrBuild>(cone_center_y)->value;
    // ... other options ...
}
// Method to set default values for conical slicing options
void PrintConfig::set_defaults()
{
    // ... other default values ...

    // Conical slicing defaults
    this->add_option_to_group(enable_conical_slicing, "Conical Slicing", opt_booleans, 1);
    this->option<ConfigOptionBoolOrBuild>(enable_conical_slicing)->value = {false};

    this->add_option_to_group(cone_angle, "Conical Slicing", opt_floats, 2);
    this->option<ConfigOptionFloatOrBuild>(cone_angle)->value = {45.0}; // Default cone angle
    this->option<ConfigOptionFloatOrBuild>(cone_angle)->min = 0.0;
    this->option<ConfigOptionFloatOrBuild>(cone_angle)->max = 90.0;

    this->add_option_to_group(cone_center_x, "Conical Slicing", opt_floats, 3);
    this->option<ConfigOptionFloatOrBuild>(cone_center_x)->value = {0.0}; // Default center X

    this->add_option_to_group(cone_center_y, "Conical Slicing", opt_floats, 4);
    this->option<ConfigOptionFloatOrBuild>(cone_center_y)->value = {0.0}; // Default center Y

    // ... other default values ...
}

8. Nozzle Collision Detection (High Priority):

Implement a basic collision check in ConicalSlicer::sliceLayerConically:
Before generating a toolpath point:
Create a simplified representation of the nozzle (e.g., a cylinder or cone) at the intended location.
Check if this nozzle representation intersects with any previously generated toolpaths on the current or previous layers. You might need to store a simplified representation of the toolpaths for efficient collision checking.
If a collision is detected:
Try to adjust the toolpath slightly (e.g., move to the side, change the angle).
If adjustment is not possible, you might need to skip the point or split the segment, which can get quite complex.
As a last resort, you could switch back to planar slicing for that specific layer or region, though this is not ideal.
Start simple: Begin with a very basic collision check, even if it's not perfect. You can refine it later.
9. Extrusion Adjustment for Conical Layers:

In ConicalSlicer::sliceLayerConically:
When you transform the toolpath points, you'll also need to adjust the extrusion amounts to compensate for the change in layer height and the angle of the layer.
Calculate a scaling factor: This factor will depend on the cone angle and possibly other factors. A simple approximation could be 1 / cos(cone_angle).
Apply the scaling factor: Multiply the extrusion amount for each segment by the scaling factor. This might involve modifying the ExtrusionLoop or ExtrusionPath objects, or adjusting the E values in the generated G-code.

Nozzle Collision Detection (High Priority):



Implement a basic collision check in ConicalSlicer::sliceLayerConically:Before generating a toolpath point:Create a simplified representation of the nozzle (e.g., a cylinder or cone) at the intended location.

Check if this nozzle representation intersects with any previously generated toolpaths on the current or previous layers. You might need to store a simplified representation of the toolpaths for efficient collision checking.

If a collision is detected:Try to adjust the toolpath slightly (e.g., move to the side, change the angle).

If adjustment is not possible, you might need to skip the point or split the segment, which can get quite complex.

As a last resort, you could switch back to planar slicing for that specific layer or region, though this is not ideal.



2.0 Experimental Advanced. Lacks access to real-time info and some Gemini features.
Okay, let's implement a basic nozzle collision detection system within the ConicalSlicer::sliceLayerConically function. We'll start with a simplified approach and gradually refine it.

1. Nozzle Representation:

We'll represent the nozzle as a cylinder. This is a simplification, but it's a good starting point.
We'll need the nozzle's radius and height. For now, let's assume a height that covers a few layers (e.g., 3 times the layer height).
2. Simplified Toolpath Representation:

To make collision checks faster, we'll store a simplified representation of the toolpaths. We'll just store the endpoints of each line segment in the toolpath.
We'll need a data structure to hold these points for the current and previous layers.
3. Collision Check Algorithm:

For each new toolpath point, we'll:
Create a cylinder representing the nozzle at that point.
Iterate through the stored toolpath points from the current and previous layers (up to the nozzle's height).
Check if the nozzle cylinder intersects with any line segment formed by consecutive toolpath points.
Code Implementation:

A. ConicalSlicer.hpp:

C++

#ifndef CONICALSLICER_HPP
#define CONICALSLICER_HPP

#include "libslic3r.h"
#include "Point.hpp"
#include "PrintConfig.hpp"
#include "ExtrusionEntity.hpp"
#include "Geometry.hpp"

namespace Slic3r {

class Layer; // Forward declaration

class ConicalSlicer {
public:
    ConicalSlicer();
    ~ConicalSlicer();

    // Main function to perform conical slicing on a layer
    void sliceLayerConically(Layer* layer, const PrintConfig& print_config);

private:
    // ... other helper functions ...

    // Nozzle geometry for collision detection
    double nozzle_radius;
    double nozzle_height; // Total height of the nozzle cylinder
    
    // Function to check for collisions between the nozzle and previous toolpaths
    bool checkForCollision(const Point3& nozzle_center, double nozzle_radius, double nozzle_height,
                           const std::vector<std::vector<Point3>>& previous_toolpaths);
    // Function to add new toolpath points to the collision detection system
    void addToolpathPoint(const Point3& point);

    // Data structure to store simplified toolpath points for collision detection
    std::vector<std::vector<Point3>> previous_toolpaths; // outer vector is layers, inner is points in the layer
    static constexpr size_t MAX_LAYERS_TO_CHECK = 3; // Maximum number of previous layers to check for collisions
};

} // namespace Slic3r

#endif // CONICALSLICER_HPP
B. ConicalSlicer.cpp:

C++

#include "ConicalSlicer.hpp"
#include "Layer.hpp"
#include "GCode.hpp"
#include "Print.hpp"
#include "Geometry.hpp"
#include <cmath>

namespace Slic3r {

ConicalSlicer::ConicalSlicer() : nozzle_radius(0.2), nozzle_height(0.6) {} // Example nozzle dimensions
ConicalSlicer::~ConicalSlicer() {}

void ConicalSlicer::sliceLayerConically(Layer* layer, const PrintConfig& print_config) {
    // Retrieve conical slicing parameters
    double cone_angle = print_config.opt_float("cone_angle");
    double center_x = print_config.opt_float("cone_center_x");
    double center_y = print_config.opt_float("cone_center_y");

    // Adjust layer height based on the conical method
    // Iterate through regions in the layer
    for (auto region : layer->regions()) {
        // Iterate through perimeters
        for (auto perimeter : region->perimeters()) {
            // Create a copy of the perimeter points for modification
            ExtrusionPath modified_perimeter = *perimeter;
            // Iterate through each point of the perimeter
            for (size_t i = 0; i < modified_perimeter.points.size(); ++i) {
                auto& point = modified_perimeter.points[i];
                //transform each point
                Point3 transformed_point = transformToConical(
                    Point3(point.x, point.y, layer->height),
                    cone_angle,
                    center_x,
                    center_y,
                    layer->height
                );
                // Before adding the point, check for collisions
                Point3 nozzle_center = Point3(transformed_point.x(), transformed_point.y(), transformed_point.z() + nozzle_height / 2.0);
                if (checkForCollision(nozzle_center, nozzle_radius, nozzle_height, previous_toolpaths)) {
                   // Handle collision (for now, just skip the point)
                   // You might want to implement more sophisticated handling later
                   //remove point from perimeter
                    modified_perimeter.points.erase(modified_perimeter.points.begin() + i);
                    --i; // Adjust the index after erasing
                    continue;
                }

                // Update the point in the perimeter
                point.x = transformed_point.x();
                point.y = transformed_point.y();
                
                // Adjust the layer height based on the transformation
                double radius = std::sqrt(std::pow(point.x - center_x, 2) + std::pow(point.y - center_y, 2));
                layer->height = calculateConicalLayerHeight(radius, cone_angle);

                // Add the point to the collision detection system
                addToolpathPoint(transformed_point);
            }

            // Replace the original perimeter with the modified one
            *perimeter = modified_perimeter;

            // Adjust extrusion for this perimeter
            double scale_factor = std::cos(Geometry::deg2rad(cone_angle)); // Example scale factor
            for (auto& loop : perimeter->loops) {
                adjustExtrusion(&loop, scale_factor);
            }
        }

        // Similar adjustments can be made for infill and supports
        for (auto& infill : region->infill_paths()) {
            for (size_t i = 0; i < infill.points.size(); ++i) {
                auto& point = infill.points[i];
                Point3 transformed_point = transformToConical(Point3(point.x, point.y, layer->height), cone_angle, center_x, center_y, layer->height);

                // Check for collisions
                Point3 nozzle_center = Point3(transformed_point.x(), transformed_point.y(), transformed_point.z() + nozzle_height / 2.0);
                if (checkForCollision(nozzle_center, nozzle_radius, nozzle_height, previous_toolpaths)) {
                    // Handle collision
                    //remove point from infill
                    infill.points.erase(infill.points.begin() + i);
                    --i; // Adjust the index
                    continue;
                }

                point.x = transformed_point.x();
                point.y = transformed_point.y();

                // Add the point to the collision detection system
                addToolpathPoint(transformed_point);
            }
        }
    }

    // Shift the toolpath points for the next layer
    if (previous_toolpaths.size() >= MAX_LAYERS_TO_CHECK) {
        previous_toolpaths.erase(previous_toolpaths.begin());
    }
    previous_toolpaths.push_back({}); // Add an empty layer for the new points
}

Point3 ConicalSlicer::transformToConical(const Point3& point, double cone_angle, double center_x, double center_y, double layer_height_mm) {
    // Calculate radius from the center
    double radius = std::sqrt(std::pow(point.x - center_x, 2) + std::pow(point.y - center_y, 2));

    // Calculate conical layer height
    double conical_z = layer_height_mm + calculateConicalLayerHeight(radius, cone_angle);

    // Keep x and y unchanged for this example, but you'll likely modify them
    // based on the cone's geometry and the desired toolpath.
    double transformed_x = point.x;
    double transformed_y = point.y;

    return Point3(transformed_x, transformed_y, conical_z);
}

double ConicalSlicer::calculateConicalLayerHeight(double radius, double cone_angle) {
    // Calculate the layer height based on the cone angle and radius
    return radius * std::tan(Geometry::deg2rad(cone_angle));
}

void ConicalSlicer::adjustExtrusion(ExtrusionLoop* loop, double scale_factor) {
    // Adjust the extrusion for each segment of the loop
    for (auto& segment : loop->segments) {
        segment.e *= scale_factor; // Adjust extrusion amount
    }
}

bool ConicalSlicer::checkForCollision(const Point3& nozzle_center, double nozzle_radius, double nozzle_height,
                                       const std::vector<std::vector<Point3>>& previous_toolpaths) {
    // Iterate through previous layers
    for (size_t layer_idx = 0; layer_idx < previous_toolpaths.size(); ++layer_idx) {
        const auto& layer_points = previous_toolpaths[layer_idx];

        // Iterate through segments in the layer
        for (size_t i = 0; i < layer_points.size() - 1; ++i) {
            const Point3& p1 = layer_points[i];
            const Point3& p2 = layer_points[i + 1];

            // Check for intersection between the nozzle cylinder and the segment
            if (Geometry::segmentCylinderIntersection(p1, p2, nozzle_center, nozzle_radius, nozzle_height)) {
                return true; // Collision detected
            }
        }
    }

    return false; // No collision
}

void ConicalSlicer::addToolpathPoint(const Point3& point) {
    if (!previous_toolpaths.empty()) {
        previous_toolpaths.back().push_back(point);
    }
}
} // namespace Slic3r

D. Geometry.cpp:

C++

#include "Geometry.hpp"
#include <cmath>

namespace Slic3r {
namespace Geometry {
// ... other functions ...

bool segmentCylinderIntersection(const Point3& p1, const Point3& p2, const Point3& nozzle_center, double nozzle_radius, double nozzle_height) {
    // Simplified collision check between a line segment (p1, p2) and a cylinder (nozzle)

    // Project segment onto the XY plane
    Point p1_xy(p1.x(), p1.y());
    Point p2_xy(p2.x(), p2.y());

    // Check if the segment is within the nozzle's height range
    double min_z = nozzle_center.z() - nozzle_height / 2.0;
    double max_z = nozzle_center.z() + nozzle_height / 2.0;

    if ((p1.z() < min_z && p2.z() < min_z) || (p1.z() > max_z && p2.z() > max_z)) {
        return false; // Segment is outside the nozzle's height range
    }

    // Calculate distance from segment to the nozzle center (in XY plane)
    double dist = Geometry::distance_to_segment(Point(nozzle_center.x(), nozzle_center.y()), p1_xy, p2_xy);

    return dist <= nozzle_radius;
}
// ... other functions ...
} // namespace Geometry
} // namespace Slic3r

Okay, let's implement Geometry::segmentCylinderIntersection and refine the collision handling in ConicalSlicer.

1. Geometry::segmentCylinderIntersection Implementation

We'll implement a method to check if a line segment intersects a cylinder. We'll use a common approach that involves finding the closest point on the line segment to the cylinder's axis and then checking if that point is within the cylinder's radius and height.

Here's the C++ code for Geometry.cpp and the declaration in Geometry.hpp:

Geometry.hpp:

C++

#ifndef SLIC3R_GEOMETRY_HPP
#define SLIC3R_GEOMETRY_HPP

// ... other includes and declarations ...

namespace Slic3r {
namespace Geometry {

// ... other functions ...

// Function to check intersection between a 3D line segment and a cylinder
bool segmentCylinderIntersection(const Point3& p1, const Point3& p2, const Point3& nozzle_center,
                                 double nozzle_radius, double nozzle_height);

} // namespace Geometry
} // namespace Slic3r

#endif // SLIC3R_GEOMETRY_HPP
Geometry.cpp:

C++

#include "Geometry.hpp"
#include <cmath>
#include <algorithm>

namespace Slic3r {
namespace Geometry {

// ... other functions ...

bool segmentCylinderIntersection(const Point3& p1, const Point3& p2, const Point3& nozzle_center,
                                 double nozzle_radius, double nozzle_height) {
    // Cylinder axis defined by nozzle_center (bottom) and nozzle_top (top)
    Point3 nozzle_top = nozzle_center + Point3(0, 0, nozzle_height);

    // Vector along the segment
    Point3 d = p2 - p1;

    // Vector from segment start to cylinder axis start
    Point3 m = p1 - nozzle_center;

    // Coefficients for the quadratic equation
    double dd = d.dot(d);
    double md = m.dot(d);
    double mm = m.dot(m);

    // Calculate the closest point on the segment to the cylinder axis
    double t = -md / dd;
    t = std::max(0.0, std::min(1.0, t)); // Clamp t to the segment range [0, 1]

    // Closest point on the segment
    Point3 closest = p1 + d * t;

    // Vector from cylinder axis start to closest point
    Point3 q = closest - nozzle_center;

    // Check if the closest point is within the cylinder's height
    double nozzle_axis_height = nozzle_top.z() - nozzle_center.z();
    double projection = (closest.z() - nozzle_center.z()) / nozzle_axis_height;

    if (projection < 0.0 || projection > 1.0) {
        return false; // Closest point is outside the cylinder's height
    }

    // Check if the distance from the closest point to the cylinder axis is within the radius
    double distance_squared = q.dot(q);
    return distance_squared <= (nozzle_radius * nozzle_radius);
}

} // namespace Geometry
} // namespace Slic3r
2. Refined Collision Handling in ConicalSlicer

We'll modify ConicalSlicer::sliceLayerConically to handle collisions more intelligently. Instead of just skipping points, we'll try to adjust the toolpath slightly.

C++

// ... (inside ConicalSlicer::sliceLayerConically) ...

// Before adding the point, check for collisions
Point3 nozzle_center = Point3(transformed_point.x(), transformed_point.y(), transformed_point.z() + nozzle_height / 2.0);
if (checkForCollision(nozzle_center, nozzle_radius, nozzle_height, previous_toolpaths)) {
    // Handle collision
    bool collisionResolved = false;

    // 1. Try small adjustments (move the point slightly along the normal)
    Vec3 normal = (transformed_point - Point3(center_x, center_y, transformed_point.z())).normalized(); // Normal from center to point
    for (double offset = 0.1; offset <= nozzle_radius; offset += 0.1) { // Try offsets up to the nozzle radius
        Point3 adjusted_point = transformed_point + normal * offset;
        nozzle_center = Point3(adjusted_point.x(), adjusted_point.y(), adjusted_point.z() + nozzle_height / 2.0);

        if (!checkForCollision(nozzle_center, nozzle_radius, nozzle_height, previous_toolpaths)) {
            transformed_point = adjusted_point;
            collisionResolved = true;
            break;
        }
    }

    // 2. (Optional) Try splitting the segment if small adjustments don't work

    // 3. (Last Resort) Skip the point or switch to planar slicing (not implemented here)
    if (!collisionResolved) {
        //remove point from perimeter
         modified_perimeter.points.erase(modified_perimeter.points.begin() + i);
         --i; // Adjust the index after erasing
         continue;
    }
}

// Update the point in the perimeter (if no collision or collision resolved)
point.x = transformed_point.x();
point.y = transformed_point.y();

3. Extrusion Adjustment

We'll implement the extrusion scaling based on the cone angle.

C++

void ConicalSlicer::adjustExtrusion(ExtrusionLoop* loop, double scale_factor) {
    // Adjust the extrusion for each segment of the loop based on the scale factor
    for (ExtrusionPath& path : loop->paths) {
        for (size_t i = 0; i < path.points.size(); ++i) {
            // Retrieve the original extrusion amount
            double original_extrusion = path.e;

            // Calculate the scaled extrusion amount
            double scaled_extrusion = original_extrusion * scale_factor;

            // Update the extrusion amount in the path
            path.e = scaled_extrusion;
        }
    }
}

2. Path Smoothing

Smoothing the toolpath after adjustments can improve print quality and reduce vibrations.

Implementation Approach:

Choose an Algorithm:
Bezier Curves: A good option for creating smooth, curved paths. You can fit Bezier curves to a sequence of points.
B-Splines: Another type of spline curve that offers good control over smoothness.
Moving Average: A simpler method that averages the coordinates of nearby points to smooth out sharp angles.
Implementation:
Create a new function (e.g., smoothToolpath) that takes a ExtrusionPath or a sequence of points as input.
Apply the chosen smoothing algorithm to the points.
Update the ExtrusionPath or create a new one with the smoothed points.
Integration:
Call smoothToolpath in ConicalSlicer::sliceLayerConically after the toolpath adjustments for collisions.
Example:
C++

// Inside ConicalSlicer::sliceLayerConically, after collision handling:

// ... collision handling code ...

// Smooth the modified perimeter
smoothToolpath(&modified_perimeter); // Assuming you have a function to smooth an ExtrusionPath

// Replace the original perimeter with the modified one
*perimeter = modified_perimeter;
Example: Simple Moving Average Smoothing:

C++

#include <vector>

// ...

void smoothToolpath(ExtrusionPath* path, int window_size = 3) {
    if (path->points.size() < window_size) {
        return; // Not enough points to smooth
    }

    std::vector<Point> smoothed_points;
    smoothed_points.reserve(path->points.size());

    for (size_t i = 0; i < path->points.size(); ++i) {
        Point smoothed_point;
        int count = 0;

        for (int j = std::max(0, static_cast<int>(i) - window_size / 2); j <= std::min(static_cast<int>(path->points.size()) - 1, static_cast<int>(i) + window_size / 2); ++j) {
            smoothed_point += path->points[j];
            count++;
        }

        smoothed_points.push_back(smoothed_point / count);
    }

    path->points = smoothed_points;
}

Implementation:

nozzle_tip_radius (radius at the very tip)

Custom Nozzle Shape:
If you need to represent a custom nozzle shape, you could:
Define the shape using a series of points or a mathematical function.
Implement a custom intersection function that can handle this shape.
NozzleGeometry Class (Advanced):

4. Spatial Data Structures

Using octrees or KD-trees can significantly speed up collision detection, especially for complex models.

Implementation (Conceptual):

Choose a Library:
libigl: Provides implementations of KD-trees and other useful geometry processing tools.
CGAL: A powerful computational geometry library that includes octrees and other spatial data structures.
nanoflann: A lightweight header-only library for KD-trees.
Integration:
Build the Tree: At the beginning of each layer (or when the toolpath is significantly modified), build an octree or KD-tree from the simplified toolpath points of the current and previous layers (up to the nozzle's height).
Query the Tree: Instead of iterating through all previous toolpath points in checkForCollision, use the spatial data structure to efficiently find the points that are close to the nozzle's location. Then, perform collision checks only with those nearby points.
Example (Conceptual, using a hypothetical KDTree class):
C++

// In ConicalSlicer::sliceLayerConically:

// ...

// Build KD-tree from previous toolpath points
KDTree toolpath_tree;
for (const auto& layer_points : previous_toolpaths) {
    for (const Point3& point : layer_points) {
        toolpath_tree.addPoint(point);
    }
}

// ...

// In ConicalSlicer::checkForCollision:

// ...

// Find points near the nozzle using the KD-tree
std::vector<Point3> nearby_points = toolpath_tree.findPointsWithinRadius(nozzle_center, nozzle_radius + some_tolerance);

// Iterate through nearby points and check for collisions
for (const Point3& p1 : nearby_points) {
  //check if p1 is part of a segment by iterating through segments on the layer
  for (size_t i = 0; i < layer_points.size() - 1; ++i) {
      const Point3& p2 = layer_points[i + 1];
      if (Geometry::segmentCylinderIntersection(p1, p2, nozzle_center, nozzle_radius, nozzle_height)) {
          return true; // Collision detected
      }
   }
}
5. Performance Optimization

Profiling:
Use a profiler (e.g., gprof, valgrind, or the profiling tools in Visual Studio) to identify the most time-consuming parts of your code.
Focus Areas:
Geometry::segmentCylinderIntersection (or segmentConeIntersection): Optimize the intersection calculations. Consider using techniques like vectorization or lookup tables if applicable.
ConicalSlicer::checkForCollision: If you're not using spatial data structures, try to reduce the number of points checked for collisions. For example, you could use a coarser representation of the toolpaths for initial checks and only perform detailed checks when a potential collision is detected.
Coordinate Transformations: Optimize the transformToConical function.
Caching:
If some calculations are repeated frequently with the same inputs, consider caching the results to avoid redundant computations.
Compiler Optimizations:
Make sure you're compiling with optimization flags enabled (e.g., -O3 in GCC or Clang).
Example: Caching Conical Transformations (Illustrative)

C++

// In ConicalSlicer.hpp
class ConicalSlicer {
    // ...
private:
    std::map<std::pair<Point3, double>, Point3> transformation_cache; // Cache for storing transformations
};

// In ConicalSlicer::transformToConical
Point3 ConicalSlicer::transformToConical(const Point3& point, double cone_angle, double center_x, double center_y, double layer_height_mm) {
    // Create a key for the cache
    std::pair<Point3, double> cache_key = std::make_pair(point, layer_height_mm);

    // Check if the transformation is already cached
    if (transformation_cache.count(cache_key)) {
        return transformation_cache[cache_key];
    }

    // Perform the transformation
    // ... your transformation code ...

    // Store the result in the cache
    transformation_cache[cache_key] = transformed_point;

    return transformed_point;
}

// ...

void ConicalSlicer::sliceLayerConically(Layer* layer, const PrintConfig& print_config) {
    // ...

    // Adjust layer height based on the conical method
    // Iterate through regions in the layer
    for (auto region : layer->regions()) {
        // ...
        
        // Check for collisions and try to resolve them
        Point3 nozzle_center = Point3(transformed_point.x(), transformed_point.y(), transformed_point.z() + nozzle_height / 2.0);
        if (checkForCollision(nozzle_center, nozzle_radius, nozzle_height, previous_toolpaths)) {
            // Handle collision
            bool collisionResolved = false;

            // 1. Try small adjustments (move the point slightly along the normal)
            Vec3 normal = (transformed_point - Point3(center_x, center_y, transformed_point.z())).normalized(); // Normal from center to point
            for (double offset = 0.1; offset <= nozzle_radius; offset += 0.1) { // Try offsets up to the nozzle radius
                Point3 adjusted_point = transformed_point + normal * offset;
                nozzle_center = Point3(adjusted_point.x(), adjusted_point.y(), adjusted_point.z() + nozzle_height / 2.0);

                if (!checkForCollision(nozzle_center, nozzle_radius, nozzle_height, previous_toolpaths)) {
                    transformed_point = adjusted_point;
                    collisionResolved = true;
                    break;
                }
            }

            // 2. (Optional) Try splitting the segment if small adjustments don't work

            // 3. (Last Resort) Skip the point or switch to planar slicing (not implemented here)
            if (!collisionResolved) {
                // Switch to planar slicing for this layer
                layer->use_planar_slicing = true;
                
                // Log a warning or provide feedback to the user
                std::cout << "Warning: Collision detected on layer " << layer->print_z << ". Switching to planar slicing." << std::endl;
                
                // Exit the loop since we've switched to planar slicing for the entire layer
                break;
            }
        }

        // ...

Smoothing.cpp:

C++

#include "Smoothing.hpp"
#include <vector>

namespace Slic3r {

void smoothToolpath(ExtrusionPath* path, int window_size) {
    if (path->points.size() < window_size) {
        return; // Not enough points to smooth
    }

    std::vector<Point> smoothed_points;
    smoothed_points.reserve(path->points.size());

    for (size_t i = 0; i < path->points.size(); ++i) {
        Point smoothed_point;
        int count = 0;

        for (int j = std::max(0, static_cast<int>(i) - window_size / 2);
             j <= std::min(static_cast<int>(path->points.size()) - 1, static_cast<int>(i) + window_size / 2);
             ++j) {
            smoothed_point += path->points[j];
            count++;
        }

        smoothed_points.push_back(smoothed_point / count);
    }

    path->points = smoothed_points;
}

} // namespace Slic3r

ConicalSlicer.cpp:
C++

// ...

// Initialize in constructor
ConicalSlicer::ConicalSlicer() : nozzle_tip_radius(0.1), nozzle_cone_angle(45.0), nozzle_height(0.6) {}

// ...

// Modify checkForCollision to use conical geometry (we'll need a new intersection function)
bool ConicalSlicer::checkForCollision(const Point3& nozzle_tip, double nozzle_tip_radius, double nozzle_cone_angle, double nozzle_height,
                                       const std::vector<std::vector<Point3>>& previous_toolpaths) {
    // Iterate through previous layers
    for (size_t layer_idx = 0; layer_idx < previous_toolpaths.size(); ++layer_idx) {
        const auto& layer_points = previous_toolpaths[layer_idx];

        // Iterate through segments in the layer
        for (size_t i = 0; i < layer_points.size() - 1; ++i) {
            const Point3& p1 = layer_points[i];
            const Point3& p2 = layer_points[i + 1];

            // Check for intersection between the nozzle cone and the segment
            if (Geometry::segmentConeIntersection(p1, p2, nozzle_tip, nozzle_tip_radius, nozzle_cone_angle, nozzle_height)) {
                return true; // Collision detected
            }
        }
    }

    return false; // No collision
}

9. Profiling:

Tools:
Visual Studio Profiler: If you're using Visual Studio, it has built-in profiling tools.
gprof: A command-line profiler that can be used with GCC/Clang.
Valgrind (with Callgrind): Another powerful profiling tool, especially useful for identifying memory access issues.
Process:
Compile with Profiling Flags:
For gprof, add the -pg flag to your compiler and linker options.
For Valgrind, no special flags are needed.
Run Your Code: Run your modified OrcaSlicer with a representative model and settings, including conical slicing enabled.
Analyze Results:
gprof: Generate a report using gprof <your_executable> gmon.out > analysis.txt.
Valgrind: Run with valgrind --tool=callgrind ./<your_executable>. This will generate a callgrind.out.<pid> file that you can visualize with tools like kcachegrind or qcachegrind.
Identify Bottlenecks: Look for functions that consume the most time. These are the areas where optimization will have the biggest impact.
Example Profiling with gprof (Illustrative):

Compile with -pg: Add the -pg flag to your CFLAGS and LDFLAGS in your CMakeLists.txt or build system.
Run OrcaSlicer: Run your modified OrcaSlicer with a test model.
Generate Report:
Bash

gprof <path_to_your_orcaslicer_executable> gmon.out > analysis.txt
Analyze analysis.txt: Look for functions with high percentages in the "% time" column.
Example Profiling with Valgrind and KCachegrind:

Run with Valgrind:
Bash

valgrind --tool=callgrind ./<path_to_your_orcaslicer_executable>
Visualize with KCachegrind:
Bash

kcachegrind callgrind.out.<pid>

2.0 Experimental Advanced. Lacks access to real-time info and some Gemini features.
Okay, let's integrate CGAL (Computational Geometry Algorithms Library) into the OrcaSlicer build to leverage its robust geometric algorithms for segment-cone intersection and other potentially useful operations.

Disclaimer: Integrating a large library like CGAL into an existing project is a non-trivial task. It requires careful consideration of dependencies, build system modifications, and potential code adjustments. This guide provides a general outline of the process, but you might encounter specific challenges based on your development environment and the current state of your OrcaSlicer fork.

Steps for Integrating CGAL:

1. Download and Install CGAL:

Download: Go to the CGAL website (https://www.cgal.org/) and download the latest stable release.
Installation:
Windows: CGAL provides pre-compiled libraries and headers. Follow the instructions in the CGAL documentation for installation using CMake and Visual Studio. You'll likely need to set environment variables like CGAL_DIR to point to the installation directory.
Linux/macOS: You can often install CGAL using your system's package manager (e.g., apt-get install libcgal-dev on Debian/Ubuntu). Alternatively, you can build CGAL from source using CMake.
2. Update OrcaSlicer's CMakeLists.txt:

You need to tell CMake where to find the CGAL headers and libraries.
Add the following to your src/libslic3r/CMakeLists.txt (adjust paths if necessary):
<!-- end list -->

CMake

# Find CGAL
find_package(CGAL REQUIRED)

# Include CGAL headers
include_directories(${CGAL_INCLUDE_DIRS})

# ... other parts of your CMakeLists.txt ...

# Link CGAL libraries to your target (e.g., libslic3r)

#ifndef SLIC3R_GEOMETRY_HPP
#define SLIC3R_GEOMETRY_HPP

// ... other includes and declarations ...

// Include CGAL headers (example)
#include <CGAL/Exact_predicates_inexact_constructions_kernel.h>
#include <CGAL/Segment_3.h>
#include <CGAL/Cone_3.h>
#include <CGAL/intersection.h>

namespace Slic3r {
namespace Geometry {

typedef CGAL::Exact_predicates_inexact_constructions_kernel K;
typedef K::Point_3 Point_3;
typedef K::Segment_3 Segment_3;
typedef K::Triangle_3 Triangle_3;
typedef K::Vector_3 Vector_3;
typedef K::Direction_3 Direction_3;
typedef K::Line_3 Line_3;

// ... other functions ...

// Function to check intersection between a 3D line segment and a cone using CGAL
bool segmentConeIntersection(const Point3& p1, const Point3& p2, const Point3& nozzle_tip,
                             double nozzle_tip_radius, double nozzle_cone_angle, double nozzle_height);

} // namespace Geometry
} // namespace Slic3r

#endif // SLIC3R_GEOMETRY_HPP
Geometry.cpp:

C++

#include "Geometry.hpp"
#include <cmath>
#include <algorithm>

// Include CGAL headers
#include <CGAL/Exact_predicates_inexact_constructions_kernel.h>
#include <CGAL/Segment_3.h>
#include <CGAL/intersection.h>
#include <CGAL/Origin.h>

namespace Slic3r {
namespace Geometry {

// ... other functions ...

bool segmentConeIntersection(const Point3& p1, const Point3& p2, const Point3& nozzle_tip,
                             double nozzle_tip_radius, double nozzle_cone_angle, double nozzle_height) {
    // Create CGAL points for the segment
    K::Point_3 cp1(p1.x(), p1.y(), p1.z());
    K::Point_3 cp2(p2.x(), p2.y(), p2.z());
    Segment_3 segment(cp1, cp2);

    // Create CGAL points for the nozzle cone
    K::Point_3 c_nozzle_tip(nozzle_tip.x(), nozzle_tip.y(), nozzle_tip.z());

    // Calculate the apex of the cone (where the tip would be if the cone continued)
    double cone_height_to_apex = nozzle_tip_radius / tan(deg2rad(nozzle_cone_angle / 2.0));

    // Define the direction vector of the cone axis (pointing upwards from the tip)
    Vector_3 cone_axis_direction(0, 0, 1);

    // Calculate the apex point of the cone
    K::Point_3 apex = c_nozzle_tip - cone_axis_direction * cone_height_to_apex;

    // Create a CGAL Cone
    // The radius at the base of the cone needs to be calculated considering the nozzle height
    double base_radius = (nozzle_tip_radius + nozzle_height * tan(deg2rad(nozzle_cone_angle / 2.0)));

    // Create a CGAL Cone
    // Here, we are using the two-argument constructor for CGAL::Cone_3
    // The first argument is the apex of the cone, and the second argument is the squared radius of the base
    // The direction of the cone's axis is implicitly defined by the line passing through the apex and center of the base
    // The center of the base is defined by the nozzle_tip
    K::Cone_3 cone(apex, cone_axis_direction, deg2rad(nozzle_cone_angle / 2.0));

    // Check for intersection
    auto intersection_result = CGAL::intersection(segment, cone);

    // Check if an intersection occurred
    if (intersection_result) {
        // Further check based on the type of intersection
        if (const Segment_3* segment_result = boost::get<Segment_3>(&*intersection_result)) {
            // Intersection is a segment
            return true;
        } else if (const Point_3* point_result = boost::get<Point_3>(&*intersection_result)) {
            // Intersection is a point
            return true;
        }
    }

    return false;
}

} // namespace Geometry
} // namespace Slic3r
